   <h3 class="a-spacing-small">Most Helpful Customer Reviews</h3> <div id="revMHRL" class="a-section">                            <div id="rev-dpReviewsMostHelpfulAUI-R3P4UOLX09A1UF" class="a-section"><div class="a-row a-spacing-micro"><span class="a-size-small a-color-secondary">58 of 70 people found the following review helpful</span><div class="a-icon-row a-spacing-none"><a class="a-link-normal a-text-normal a-color-base" title="4.0 out of 5 stars" href="http://www.amazon.com/review/R3P4UOLX09A1UF/ref=cm_cr_dp_title?ie=UTF8&ASIN=B00MSOND8C&nodeID=541966&store=pc"><i class="a-icon a-icon-star a-star-4"></i></a><span class="a-letter-space"></span><a class="a-link-normal a-text-normal a-color-base" href="http://www.amazon.com/review/R3P4UOLX09A1UF/ref=cm_cr_dp_title?ie=UTF8&ASIN=B00MSOND8C&nodeID=541966&store=pc"><span class="a-size-base a-text-bold">Good monitor plagued by QA problems</span></a></div><span class="a-color-secondary">         <span class="a-size-normal">     <span class="a-color-secondary">       By     </span>     <a href="/gp/pdp/profile/A3P97RWAN232K/ref=cm_cr_dp_pdp" class="noTextDecoration">Kevin Dillon</a>   </span> <span class="a-color-secondary"> on August 29, 2014</span></span></div><div id="revData-dpReviewsMostHelpfulAUI-R3P4UOLX09A1UF" class="a-row a-spacing-small">      <div class="a-section">   <span class="MHRHead">For a $800 TN panel monitor I expect near perfection with everything except for the obvious TN flaws. Unfortunately this monitor has all the negatives of a TN + other problems as well.<br><br>Lets start with the positives:<br><br>The build quality of this monitor is exceptional. The stand feels extremely sturdy, rotating the screen, pivoting it, everything feels exactly how I would expect a $800 product to feel. The face of the monitor itself is absolutely stunning as the screen stretches nearly the entire length with minimal bezel. The back is elegantly designed while maximizing airflow across the components inside. I've read that ASUS overclocked the G-Sync module inside and fitted it with a larger then standard heatsink. Regardless the monitor doesn't really get hot after long gaming sessions so their cooling design is great.<br><br>The picture quality when not in motion is also extremely good. I calibrated the screen with a Spyder3 + DispCal and honestly the end result isn't much different then how it came from the factory. The colors are excellent, far better then the BenQ 2420T I came from. Contrast isn't as good as a high end IPS but it's far better than all my previous TN panels. Horizontal viewing angles are good, vertical are meh. I expected this going into it knowing it's a TN panel though, so I wasn't surprised. 1440p @ 27" is absolutely incredible, not only does it improve the usability of Windows and apps with more space to work, but it definitely provides a greater level of detail in games.<br><br>G-Sync is also very good. Initially I had a problem getting it working. My setup is a GTX690 + 750Ti, when the 750Ti is enabled though, G-Sync caps framerates and causes severe stuttering. After disabling the 750Ti G-Sync worked beautifully.</span><span class="a-declarative" data-action="columnbalancing-showfullreview" data-columnbalancing-showfullreview="{&quot;rest&quot;:&quot; Playing Crysis 3 at a mere 40fps felt as smooth as playing other games at 100fps. Screen tearing is a thing of the past. Pretty much any game between 30-60 fps with G-Sync on turns it from a laggy mess to a fluid dream. Nvidia is constantly improving the experience via driver updates and supposedly the next driver will fix the 750Ti issue (I think this also effects other cards, not just the 750Ti but any dual nvidia card setup that isn&#39;t SLI).&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;ULMB is also a great feature. CSGO is one of my favorite games and on my 2420T lightboost simply dimmed the screen too much for my liking. While ULMB also dims the screen, it&#39;s no where near what it was on the 2420T. The image instantly becomes clearer, especially during quick motions like snapping to different positions. ULMB caps the refresh rate at 120 (the option is actually greyed out when the monitor is running at 144) but honestly the difference between 120 and 144 doesn&#39;t really effect fluidity in my experience.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Now for the negatives..&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;For starters lots (when I say lots I mean more than I&#39;ve seen with any other high profile monitor release) of people have received their monitor with dead pixels. I personally have zero on my screen but it&#39;s definitely alarming reading the reports of some people having as many as 2-3. Now I understand that on a panel this new there are going to be defects but to me the number of people that are experiencing issues is too high for a $800 monitor.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The second and probably the biggest issue I have found is something called \&quot;Pixel Inversion\&quot; others have referred it it as a couple other names. Essentially the jist of the problem is that brighter colored objects (light blues and light greens especially) become distorted when in motion. It almost looks like a checkboard pattern and can be seen here courtesy of TFT Central:&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;PG278Q: http://www.tftcentral.co.uk/images/pixperan/asus_rog_swift_pg278q.jpg&lt;br /&gt;VG278HE: http://www.tftcentral.co.uk/images/pixperan/asus_vg278he.jpg&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;In the first image on the right hand side you can see the vertical line pattern of the blurred images behind the actual rendering. This artifact occurs in nearly every game I have tested. It shows up for brief periods of time on brighter objects that are in motion. Things like muzzle flashes, explosions, or even the green names that hover above characters in MMO&#39;s (World of Warcraft to be specific). The effect at first wasn&#39;t very noticeable to me but once I started seeing it in more games it&#39;s nearly impossible to unsee. In Dota 2 for example the textures on the ground in the \&quot;radiant\&quot; base show this effect slightly during any motion of the camera at all. I&#39;ve also seen the effect in other games like Witcher 2 and Warframe. Now if I was the only one I&#39;d simply just chalk it up to bad panel but there is a thread on ASUS Rog&#39;s forums with a number of people experiencing the issue. I also think more people will report the issue once they&#39;ve spent some time with the monitor.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;http://rog.asus.com/forum/showthread.php?50004-PG278Q-vertical-stripes-esp.-in-3D-mode.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Third, apparently some of our European friends who have had access to this monitor for some time report that initially the monitor was excellent but started displaying distorted text and \&quot;halos\&quot; around objects several weeks after purchase. Again this isn&#39;t one person but several reporting it.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Overall I think this can be an excellent monitor if ASUS sorts out the QA issues. When you pay $800 for a monitor you expect that the product you receive will be reasonably tested for things like dead pixels, inversion and longevity issues, especially when this is a monitor that was initially supposed to be released in March. When you couple the awkward NA launch of this monitor with the QA issues it definitely leaves a poor taste in people&#39;s mouths. I&#39;m not even sure if I should RMA mine because the pixel inversion problem seems to be an intrinsic property of the panel and not simply a one off thing. I might just return it all together and wait for ASUS to sort out the issues.&quot;}"><a class="a-link-normal" The gaming on this thing was great (I had all monitors for a combined time of probably one-two weeks). ULMB is very noticeable when reading scrolling text/images (go to blurbusters and do one of their benchmarks) and definitely eliminates blurring. G-Sync is great for any game where you don't need utter elimination of blurring, but you want smoothness of gameplay (ie no stutter and tearing). It works beautifully in AC4.<br><br>Now, let's get into the cons.<br><br>Here are the cons:<br>- Usual TN panel cons (I list those at the bottom)<br>- Only one input. You're not doing any console gaming on this, ever. Unless it's through an emulator.<br>- Various Nvidia driver problems. I occasional got "Signal out of Range", my screen just turning black, blue screens, and other various fun little things happening, especially on my first panel.<br>- The anti-glare coating is bad. It's not as bad as the U2711 from Dell (which I returned because of the coating alone), but it is bad. ASUS should have used the Qnix anti-glare coating for this monitor, which was literally one of the most perfect balances I have ever seen.<br><br>Oh, and the deal breaker:<br>- QUALITY CONTROL!!<br>- QUALITY CONTROL!!<br>- QUALITY CONTROL!!<br>(three lines for three trials)<br><br>The con is so major and pissed me off so much across my THREE TRIALS at getting a decent panel that I believe this actually literally deserves to take off four stars. I only took off three because gaming on it was at least good.</span><span class="a-declarative" data-action="columnbalancing-showfullreview" data-columnbalancing-showfullreview="{&quot;rest&quot;:&quot; I was trying to get this as my birthday present (well, from me to me) and it has been nothing but frustration.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Allow me to recount all three of my experiences (two are from Fry&#39;s one is from the one I just got from Amazon as my third try at this)&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Panel 1:&lt;br /&gt;This thing came with 2 messed up pixels and a very mediocre backlight uniformity... but okay, alright, I was willing to deal with that because I knew I had bad luck with LCD&#39;s in general. So, I stick with it. 2-3 days later, while I&#39;m playing Payday 2 the backlight just starts flickering CONSTANTLY on me while I&#39;m actually playing. I alt tab out and all of my text is fuzzy and messed up. I tried driver reinstalls and everything under the sun, nothing helps. I read on the ROG forums at ASUS, and someone else has the same problem. And if you go over there now, you&#39;ll see more and more panels are developing this issue after a certain amount of time. Luckily mine just happened early.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Lesson learned: Make sure to burn your panel in early, especially if you&#39;re working off of a 15 day return policy. Just leave the game running.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Panel 2:&lt;br /&gt;Even more noticeable stuck pixels (according to Wikipedia dead pixels on a TN are stuck white, so in reality it was probably a dead pixel). The backlight wasn&#39;t anything great. But okay, okay. I was willing to deal with this. But there&#39;s this annoying 1-2 inch \&quot;scar\&quot; in the backlight on the right side that&#39;s plainly visible on any straight background. Not just black, any straight background. Overall I couldn&#39;t see as often during gaming but I really just couldn&#39;t deal with it being there considering this it was going to be my daily driver.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Lesson learned: Yeah, stuck pixels and backlight bleed isn&#39;t the worst that could happen to you (read about more on the ROG Forums, tons of fun to be had!).&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Panel 3:&lt;br /&gt;I just got this about an hour or two ago. I stopped counting at about 8 messed up pixels, but on a cursory scan I could see more. I estimate around 15. What panel grade is this? F-?&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Lesson learned: for panel defect count, the sky is the limit.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;This has been nothing but frustration. Now I know that most of the price premium is pretty much the extra features. None of it went into quality control. I mean I&#39;m even willing to let the perfectionist inside of me be put aside for the greater good, but this is too much. Especially for an 800$ TN panel. It&#39;s back to my Qnix I guess, which is ironically better than any mainstream monitor I have ever bought.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Oh, and here are the TN panel cons for those interested:&lt;br /&gt;- Mediocre horizontal viewing angles&lt;br /&gt;- Very bad vertical viewing angles (it might get you to stop slouching in that chair)&lt;br /&gt;- Although this is an 8 bit panel, the colors are still worse than on my 300$ Qnix (so was everything besides gaming performance, come to think of it).&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;I&#39;m really considering being the first 1 star reviewer because of how much this whole ordeal annoyed me. Unfortunately that means no shots of the one I got from Amazon. This monitor was AMAZING.  Beautiful picture.  G-Sync is is pretty awesome technology as well, and having it in a 144hz screen with the 1440p resolution definitely makes this monitor worth it's price tag.  Especially since it's the only one of it's type.  I'd expect to see the price on this drop as soon as we see any type of competition from other manufacturers.<br><br>However, I would not recommend buying one at this time.  My monitor was dead in less than a month.  I think the technology is great, but possibly rushed to market.  It seems like there is some insane demand for this monitor and/or some major quality control issues or even a design flaw.  I see several other reviewers saying they've also experienced defects in the same model.  Seeing how this monitor appears to be unavailable makes me wonder if ASUS has found a problem in the design or engineering.  I will probably buy another one, but I'm going to wait to see if the reliability gets any better first. </div>  </div>        <a class="a-link-normal comment-link" title="0" Please try again   </div></div></div> </span>    </div>            <div id="rev-dpReviewsMostHelpfulAUI-RTMBYABJ4NCQR" class="a-section"><div class="a-row a-spacing-micro"><span class="a-size-small a-color-secondary">5 of 6 people found the following review helpful</span><div class="a-icon-row a-spacing-none"><a class="a-link-normal a-text-normal a-color-base" title="5.0 out of 5 stars" href="http://www.amazon.com/review/RTMBYABJ4NCQR/ref=cm_cr_dp_title?ie=UTF8&ASIN=B00MSOND8C&nodeID=541966&store=pc"><i class="a-icon a-icon-star a-star-5"></i></a><span class="a-letter-space"></span><a class="a-link-normal a-text-normal a-color-base" href="http://www.amazon.com/review/RTMBYABJ4NCQR/ref=cm_cr_dp_title?ie=UTF8&ASIN=B00MSOND8C&nodeID=541966&store=pc"><span class="a-size-base a-text-bold">A G-sync monitor is everything they advertise.</span></a></div><span class="a-color-secondary">         <span class="a-size-normal">     <span class="a-color-secondary">       By     </span>     <a href="/gp/pdp/profile/A37CDON8E7QXH0/ref=cm_cr_dp_pdp" class="noTextDecoration">Jon Snow</a>   </span> <span class="a-color-secondary"> on October 13, 2014</span></span></div><div id="revData-dpReviewsMostHelpfulAUI-RTMBYABJ4NCQR" class="a-row a-spacing-small">      <div class="a-section">   <span class="MHRHead">First, I have to start off by saying they must have released a bad batch of monitors because there are a few people out there indicating out of range errors (Asus ROG site shows the Nvidia 344.11 driver will clear this up if you have any issue) and dead pixel issues.  This is definitely not the case with mine.  Mine has worked from day one without issues, but I also know what it's like to get a bad product.<br><br>Now the review...<br>A G-sync monitor is everything they advertise.  Frame rates can vary greatly while gaming on a PC.  As gamers, we game well above the 60fps/60Hz mark and try to keep our frame rates up there to reduce the effects of perceptible tearing.  The faster the frames refresh on the screen, the less we notice tearing.  Also, we don't want to turn on V-sync because it introduces quite a bit of input lag and can also produce a flutter effect to the displayed image.  Until G-sync, monitors were locked at static refresh rates as a leftover to old tube technology.  G-sync lets the frame rate of the video card match the refresh rate of the monitor one-to-one.  The monitor's refresh rate changes dynamically to match the frame rate of your video card solution.  This means, a full frame is always displayed and tearing is eliminated.<br><br>I started with a setup that had two 780s and a 120Hz monitor (This is a great setup in its own right!).  I ran a 120Hz monitor and hooked up the PG278Q 144Hz monitor.  I switched the primary monitor to test each.<br><br>At first, I was underwhelmed.  The 120Hz and the G-sync both provided a great gaming experience.  Both provided smooth video.  Clear images.  The Asus monitor has great colors.  The colors of the PG278Q are nearly the same as the PB278Q PLS monitor I use for work.</span><span class="a-declarative" data-action="columnbalancing-showfullreview" data-columnbalancing-showfullreview="{&quot;rest&quot;:&quot;  The viewing angles aren&#39;t great on the TN panel, but since you sit directly in front of it to game or work, it&#39;s a non-issue.  I guess if you were gaming from the awkward position of the next desk over or standing while looking down at the monitor without tilting it appropriately, it might bug you.  Contrast is better on most TN panels when compared to IPS panels under the $1000 mark.  The contrast on this monitor is among the best of any TN panels.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;With the PG278Q, the moment of clarity came when I disabled SLI (disabling one of your cards is really an emotional process when you&#39;re an enthusiast/gamer) to test the effectiveness of G-sync at lower framerates (consistently lower than 60fps) and ran with a single 780 while keeping the details cranked.  Tearing became an issue on my 120Hz monitor at lower frame rates, but whether my frame rate was 110 or 30, on the G-sync monitor, you can&#39;t tell because the video is smooth at any frame rate.  The motion is smooth from one frame rate to the next.  The fps number simply becomes arbitrary with G-sync.  The only time I noticed any stutter was when the frame rates hit the low 20s during a visually demanding part of Tomb Raider (yes, with TressFX on and details to the max).  This episode was brief, but I was able to see stutter as if a flip book cartoon was being flipped too slowly.  The GPU just wasn&#39;t pumping out the frames.  At this low framerate (low 20fps), human perception is everything to do with seeing lack of frames.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Now I realize that if I can get a single GPU solution that allows me to keep my absolute minimum frame rates in the games I play in the high 20s or above, with a G-sync monitor, I can run with one video card and still maintain the max details.  So I picked up a single GTX 980 and sold my 780s.  This setup does everything I expected.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;If I didn&#39;t have a G-sync monitor, I&#39;d continue to run with 2 high-end GPUs and a 120Hz+ monitor.  Now that I have the PG278Q, I will only ever have to run with one.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;I won&#39;t take a star away for having only the displayPort connection since I only ever hook a PC to my gaming monitor anyway.  I also have a 7.1 surround system hooked up to my PC so sound is not even a consideration for me.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;G-sync technology is amazing. If you consider how long a monitor lasts, this monitor will actually save you money (mind you I would never pay more than MSRP) in the long run since you&#39;ll only ever need a single GPU.  More models will be released by years end and lists of G-sync monitors - existing and upcoming - are available on the web.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;If I didn&#39;t have a G-sync capable video card, I wouldn&#39;t even consider this monitor.  It is a great 144Hz monitor, but the premium you pay is only of value to those with G-sync capable video cards.  On the other hand, I definitely recommend this to anyone with a G-sync enabled video card(s).&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Dynamic-refresh technology is definitely a step in the right direction.&quot;}"><a class="a-link-normal" class="a-letter-space"></span>Read more &rsaquo;</a></span> </div>  </div>             <a class="a-link-normal comment-link" title="6"
